## Attempt 7

**System Prompt:**
> You are a world-class International Mathematical Olympiad (IMO) competitor. The final answer must be a non-negative integer between 0 and 99999. You must place the final integer answer inside \boxed{}.

**User Prompt:**
> Let $\mathcal{F}$ be the set of functions $\alpha \colon \mathbb{Z}\to \mathbb{Z}$ for which there are only finitely many $n \in \mathbb{Z}$ such that $\alpha(n) \neq 0$. 

For two functions $\alpha$ and $\beta$ in $\mathcal{F}$, define their product $\alpha\star\beta$ to be $\sum\limits_{n\in\mathbb{Z}} \alpha(n)\cdot \beta(n)$. Also, for $n\in\mathbb{Z}$, define a shift operator $S_n \colon \mathcal{F}\to \mathcal{F}$ by $S_n(\alpha)(t)=\alpha(t+n)$ for all $t \in \mathbb{Z}$.

A function $\alpha \in \mathcal{F}$ is called \emph{shifty} if 
\begin{itemize}
    \item $\alpha(m)=0$ for all integers $m<0$ and $m>8$ and
    \item There exists $\beta \in \mathcal{F}$ and integers $k \neq l$ such that for all $n \in \mathbb{Z}$
    \begin{equation*}
        S_n(\alpha)\star\beta =
        \begin{cases}
            1 & n \in \{k,l\} \\
            0 & n \not \in \{k,l\}
        \end{cases}
        \; .
    \end{equation*}
\end{itemize}
How many shifty functions are there in $\mathcal{F}$? You have access to `math`, `numpy` and `sympy` to solve the problem.

### Turn 0 - Model Response:
analysisWe need to compute the number of functions α: Z → Z with finite support contained in [0,8] (since α(m)=0 for m<0 and m>8). So support subset of {0,1,...,8}. Also values can be any integer (not just 0/1). The definition of shifty requires existence of β∈ℱ and distinct integers k,l such that for all n, S_n(α) ⋆ β equals 1 when n equals k or l, and equals 0 otherwise. Here the dot product (⋆) between two functions is sum over t ∈ ℤ of α(t)β(t). Since both have finite support, it's finite sum.

Define α with support inside [0,8]; β may be any function with finite support, any integers.

Define S_n(α) (shifted α) as t↦α(t+n). Then its support is {t: t+n∈[0,8]} = [-n,8-n] intersect ℤ. So S_n(α) is α shifted left by n? Actually S_n: α(t+n) = shift by -n? Let's compute: S_n(α)(t) = α(t+n). So to get original α at t = α(t). Then S_n(α) evaluated at t corresponds to original at index t+n. So it's shift to left if n positive? For n positive, S_n(α)(t)=α(t+n) meaning for t fixed, you look at α at larger index, so shape moves leftwards (like shifting graph left). It's similar to translation: α(t) → α(t-n) after shift? But typical shift operator: (S_n α)(t)=α(t-n) to shift right. But here they defined with +n. Anyway.

Now define product with β: S_n(α) ⋆ β = sum_t α(t+n) * β(t). This is convolution? Indeed, define (α * β)(-n?) Actually discrete convolution (α * β)(n) = sum_t α(t) β(n-t). Here sum_t α(t+n) β(t) = sum_t α(t+n) β(t). Change variable u = t+n, t = u-n: sum_u α(u) β(u-n). That's (α ⋆ β')(?) Usually convolution (α ⋆ β)(n)=∑α(i)β(n-i). So ours is sum_{i} α(i) β(i-n) = (α ⋆ β_rev)(n) where β_rev(k)=β(-k). But not needed; we just need the existence of β and two n such that this inner product equals 1 at exactly two shift positions.

Hence the condition says there is a β such that the inner product sequence f(n) = S_n(α)⋆β equals exactly the indicator of {k,l}, with value 1 at those two n and 0 elsewhere. So essentially we need to find α such that there exists β (finite support) such that the sequence of cross-correlation (or convolution) yields exactly two 1's at distinct n.

Important: α is only nonzero within [0,8]; support size at most 9. β can be arbitrary finite support (maybe bigger).

We need to count α's for which such a β (and distinct k,l) exist.

Observation: The cross-correlation (or convolution) of finite sequences yields a finite sequence as well: Since α and β have finite support, f(n) = ∑ α(t+n) β(t) is nonzero only for n such that the supports overlap: i.e., there exists t with α(t+n)≠0 and β(t)≠0. This means n belongs to range (support(α) - support(β)) (differences). Since α support within [0,8], support(β) maybe some subset of Z. So f is zero for all n outside this finite difference set. We require that f equals 0 for all but two integer n, where it's 1.

Thus we need α,β such that f has exactly two positions with value 1, all others 0. In particular, f has finite support of size 2.

Thus α and β must be such that correlation yields exactly two nonzero entries, each equal to 1.

Thus this reduces to counting α such that there exists β producing that pattern.

Observation: Since α is zero outside [0,8], we may restrict β's support to some interval as well because otherwise shift values produce zeros outside.

But we could possibly pick β to be something like α's "inverse" with a linear combination to produce δ's. This reminiscent of sequence being "sharp" under convolution: ability to produce two point masses.

Because convolution of two sequences yields a sum of products; each term at shift n includes contributions from all overlapping pairs. For f(n) to be 1 at n = k,l, we need for each of those n: ∑_{i} α(i) β(i-n) = 1, and for all other n: sum = 0.

Thus the system of equations for β entries (unknown integers) is a linear equation (over ℤ) with unknown integer variables (β's values at its support positions). We need existence of integer solution β.

We need to count α with some support within [0,8] such that there exist distinct k,l and integer vector β solving these constraints.

So this is like solving linear equations: For each n ∈ ℤ, ∑_{i=0}^8 α(i) β(i-n) = δ_{n,k} + δ_{n,l}, where δ denotes Kronecker delta. Since only finite many n produce nonzero terms (where supports intersect), we need only consider n such that there is some i where β(i-n) may be nonzero i.e., i-n is in support(β). Let support(β) subset of some finite set B. Then n = i - b, for i ∈ support(α), b ∈ B. So the set of relevant n is differences between support(α) and support(β). The number of such n is at most |support(α)|·|support(β)|.

Our condition is that among these differences, exactly two produce sum 1, all others zero.

Thus the pattern is like α and β are "orthogonal" except at two shifts.

We also need distinct k,l. Could be any integers; not restricted to [0,8] etc.

Since α is supported in [0,8], we could shift β accordingly to get these differences.

Observation: If we set β = e_m (i.e., β(t) = 1 for t = m, else 0). Then f(n) = α(m + n)? Let's compute: S_n(α)⋆β = ∑_{t} α(t + n) β(t) = α(m + n) β(m)?? Actually β(t) = 1 only at t=m, else 0. We need to count all α for which there exists some β (any integers) and distinct k,l s.t. the cross-correlation yields two 1's. This is like α is "two-pointwise independent" under some convolution.

The problem is similar to find α for which there exists β such that the convolution α ⋆ β (or correlation) is a vector of Hamming weight 2, each entry 1. That is, α and β as integer sequences produce a "two-sparse" convolution.

Let's compute: Φ_p(x) = x^{p-1}+x^{p-2}+...+1, constant term 1. For p^k (k>1), e.g., Φ_{p^2}(x) = x^{p(p-1)} + x^{p(p-2)} + ... + 1? Actually formula: Φ_{p^k}(x) = Φ_p(x^{p^{k-1}}). For p=2, k=2: Φ_4(x) = x^2 + 1, constant term 1. So constant term is 1 for all n>1. So D has constant term 1 (if no sign factor). Multiply by -1 flips constant term -1.

Thus D is polynomial with nonnegative exponents.

So the lcm may have a higher 2-adic exponent than each a_i if they differ. So we need a_i's to have same 2-adic valuation; else cannot get a single d satisfying condition for both.

So d must be odd. For Φ_4 (m=4): a=2, need 2|d, and 4 ∤ d => d ≡ 2 mod 4 (i.e., d even but not divisible by 4). That's incompatible because d must be odd for Φ_2 but even for Φ_4. So they cannot both divide same binomial. So the set of allowed cyclotomic factors must have same 2-adic valuation in a_i's (i.e., same parity of d). So only factors where a_i have same exponent of 2 may appear together.

Thus enumeration is more subtle.

Then we can choose d = L (lcm of a_i) such that d / a_i is odd for each (ensured by same 2-adic exponent). Then all these factors divide x^{d}+1. So D is valid. Moreover, any product of Φ_{2a} with the a_i having same v_2 exponent is allowed.

Now we need to generate all such products where sum of degrees ≤8.

We'll do enumeration programmatically: iterate over d from 1 up to some maximum (maybe 30?). For each d, factor x^{d}+1 into cyclotomic polynomials (using sympy factorization). Then get list of divisors (subsets of factors) (including sign ±) and compute degree. Collect all unique polynomials (up to sign?). Actually sign will be accounted later, we can collect the polynomial (monic) factor ignoring sign.

But factoring x^{d}+1 for each d up to maybe 30? That's not huge. We'll compute all divisors (distinct) of any x^{d}+1 for d up to 30, then filter degree ≤8. Then compute number of unique polynomials (monic, ignoring sign). Then compute total α count with shift p (0..8-deg) and sign factor ±1.

Let's implement via python.assistantanalysis to=python codeimport sympy as sp

x = sp.symbols('x')
max_d = 30  # maybe enough
divisor_polys = set()

def poly_key(poly):
    # Return tuple of coefficients from lowest degree to highest for unique representation
    # make monic? but we allow sign -1 separate later.
    poly = sp.expand(poly)
    # convert to integer coefficients
    coeffs = sp.Poly(poly, x).all_coeffs()  # from highest degree down
    # convert to tuple from lowest degree
    coeffs_rev = tuple(reversed([int(c) for c in coeffs]))
    # ensure leading coefficient positive? For uniqueness we will store monic positive leading coefficient.
    # But we will later multiply sign.
    # Let's store monic version: divide by leading coefficient (which is 1 or -1)
    # Actually cyclotomic polynomials are monic with leading coeff 1. So any divisor will have leading coeff ±1.
    # To have a standard representation, we keep as is.
    return coeffs_rev

# enumerate all d
for d in range(1, max_d+1):
    poly = sp.expand(x**d + 1)
    # factor over integers into irreducible factors (including cyclotomic)
    factorization = sp.factor(poly, modulus=None)  # sympy factor over Z
    # factor returns product expression
    # get list of (factor, exponent)
    facs = sp.factor_list(poly)  # returns (coeff, [(factor, exp), ...])
    coeff, facs_list = facs
    # facs_list is list of (poly, exponent)
    # exponent should be 1.
    # Collect all possible subsets (including empty)
    n_facs = len(facs_list)
    # generate subsets
    for mask in range(1 << n_facs):
        # start with 1
        sub = 1
        for i, (f, exp) in enumerate(facs_list):
            if mask & (1 << i):
                sub *= f
        # store polynomial (monic)
        # ensure sub is integer polynomial
        sub = sp.expand(sub)
        # ignore sign? We'll keep monic version; sub's leading coefficient is 1.
        # It may be 1 for empty set.
        # compute degree
        deg = sp.degree(sub, x)
        if deg <= 8:
            # store representation
            divisor_polys.add(poly_key(sub))
len(divisor_polys)


### Turn 1 - Python Call:
```python
import sympy as sp

x = sp.symbols('x')
max_d = 30  # maybe enough
divisor_polys = set()

def poly_key(poly):
    # Return tuple of coefficients from lowest degree to highest for unique representation
    # make monic? but we allow sign -1 separate later.
    poly = sp.expand(poly)
    # convert to integer coefficients
    coeffs = sp.Poly(poly, x).all_coeffs()  # from highest degree down
    # convert to tuple from lowest degree
    coeffs_rev = tuple(reversed([int(c) for c in coeffs]))
    # ensure leading coefficient positive? For uniqueness we will store monic positive leading coefficient.
    # But we will later multiply sign.
    # Let's store monic version: divide by leading coefficient (which is 1 or -1)
    # Actually cyclotomic polynomials are monic with leading coeff 1. So any divisor will have leading coeff ±1.
    # To have a standard representation, we keep as is.
    return coeffs_rev

# enumerate all d
for d in range(1, max_d+1):
    poly = sp.expand(x**d + 1)
    # factor over integers into irreducible factors (including cyclotomic)
    factorization = sp.factor(poly, modulus=None)  # sympy factor over Z
    # factor returns product expression
    # get list of (factor, exponent)
    facs = sp.factor_list(poly)  # returns (coeff, [(factor, exp), ...])
    coeff, facs_list = facs
    # facs_list is list of (poly, exponent)
    # exponent should be 1.
    # Collect all possible subsets (including empty)
    n_facs = len(facs_list)
    # generate subsets
    for mask in range(1 << n_facs):
        # start with 1
        sub = 1
        for i, (f, exp) in enumerate(facs_list):
            if mask & (1 << i):
                sub *= f
        # store polynomial (monic)
        # ensure sub is integer polynomial
        sub = sp.expand(sub)
        # ignore sign? We'll keep monic version; sub's leading coefficient is 1.
        # It may be 1 for empty set.
        # compute degree
        deg = sp.degree(sub, x)
        if deg <= 8:
            # store representation
            divisor_polys.add(poly_key(sub))
len(divisor_polys)

```

### Turn 12 - Python Call:
```python
def divides_any(dmax, poly):
    for d in range(1,dmax+1):
        if sp.rem(x**d+1, poly, x) == 0:
            return True, d
    return False, None

poly_test = sp.expand((x+1)*(x**4 - x**2 + 1))
divides_any(30, poly_test)

```

However we can be smarter: we already enumerated α candidates: each A(x) = ε x^{p} D(x). Let's generate all such α and verify via constructing β as described. Ensure there are exactly 160 distinct α.

assistantanalysis to=python codedef generate_shifty_alphas():
    alphas = set()
    # D list as polynomials (Sympy)
    D_polys = [sum(c*x**i for i,c in enumerate(coeffs)) for coeffs in sorted_polys]
    for D in D_polys:
        degD = sp.degree(D, x)
        for p in range(0, 9 - degD):
            for sign in [1, -1]:
                A = sign * x**p * D
                # convert to tuple of coefficients from 0..8
                coeffs = [int(sp.expand(A).coeff(x, i)) for i in range(0,9)]
                alphas.add(tuple(coeffs))
    return alphas

alphas = generate_shifty_alphas()
len(alphas)


### Turn 14 - Python Call:
```python
def generate_shifty_alphas():
    alphas = set()
    # D list as polynomials (Sympy)
    D_polys = [sum(c*x**i for i,c in enumerate(coeffs)) for coeffs in sorted_polys]
    for D in D_polys:
        degD = sp.degree(D, x)
        for p in range(0, 9 - degD):
            for sign in [1, -1]:
                A = sign * x**p * D
                # convert to tuple of coefficients from 0..8
                coeffs = [int(sp.expand(A).coeff(x, i)) for i in range(0,9)]
                alphas.add(tuple(coeffs))
    return alphas

alphas = generate_shifty_alphas()
len(alphas)

```

**System Prompt:**
> You are a world-class International Mathematical Olympiad (IMO) competitor. The final answer must be a non-negative integer between 0 and 99999. You must place the final integer answer inside \boxed{}.

**User Prompt:**
> Let $\mathcal{F}$ be the set of functions $\alpha \colon \mathbb{Z}\to \mathbb{Z}$ for which there are only finitely many $n \in \mathbb{Z}$ such that $\alpha(n) \neq 0$. 

For two functions $\alpha$ and $\beta$ in $\mathcal{F}$, define their product $\alpha\star\beta$ to be $\sum\limits_{n\in\mathbb{Z}} \alpha(n)\cdot \beta(n)$. Also, for $n\in\mathbb{Z}$, define a shift operator $S_n \colon \mathcal{F}\to \mathcal{F}$ by $S_n(\alpha)(t)=\alpha(t+n)$ for all $t \in \mathbb{Z}$.

A function $\alpha \in \mathcal{F}$ is called \emph{shifty} if 
\begin{itemize}
    \item $\alpha(m)=0$ for all integers $m<0$ and $m>8$ and
    \item There exists $\beta \in \mathcal{F}$ and integers $k \neq l$ such that for all $n \in \mathbb{Z}$
    \begin{equation*}
        S_n(\alpha)\star\beta =
        \begin{cases}
            1 & n \in \{k,l\} \\
            0 & n \not \in \{k,l\}
        \end{cases}
        \; .
    \end{equation*}
\end{itemize}
How many shifty functions are there in $\mathcal{F}$? You have access to `math`, `numpy` and `sympy` to solve the problem.

Let's try.assistantanalysis to=python codeimport sympy as sp, itertools, math, collections, sys, json, random, math, functools, fractions, decimal, fractions, collections, itertools, math
x=sp.Symbol('x')
def divisors_of_one_plus_xd(d):
    # factor polynomial over Z
    f = sp.expand(1 + x**d)
    fac = sp.factor(f, modulus=None)  # factor over integers
    # sympy factor returns product of irreducible factors
    # need to get list of irreducible factors with multiplicities
    # we can use sp.factor_list
    faclist = sp.factor_list(f)[1]  # returns [(factor,exp), ...]
    # Each factor is primitive with integer coefficients
    factors = [ (sp.expand(fac[0]), fac[1]) for fac in faclist ]
    # Since all exponents are 1 (squarefree), but we handle general.
    # Generate all subsets of factors (including empty -> 1)
    all_divisors=set()
    # We'll produce monic divisors? Not necessarily monic; but each factor might have leading coefficient 1.
    # Multiply chosen factors (with multiplicity 1)
    for r in range(len(factors)+1):
        for comb in itertools.combinations(factors, r):
            poly = sp.Integer(1)
            for fpoly,exp in comb:
                poly = sp.expand(poly * fpoly)
            all_divisors.add(sp.expand(poly))
    return all_divisors

def compute_D(maxd=30):
    D=set()
    for d in range(1, maxd+1):
        for p in divisors_of_one_plus_xd(d):
            deg = sp.degree(p)
            if deg <= 8:
                # primitive: gcd of coefficients =1
                coeffs = sp.Poly(p, x).all_coeffs()
                # compute gcd of ints
                g=0
                for c in coeffs:
                    g=math.gcd(g, int(abs(c)))
                if g==1:  # primitive (including ±1)
                    # store canonical representation: maybe as tuple of coeffs
                    D.add(sp.expand(p))
    return D

D=compute_D(30)
len(D)


### Turn 0 - Python Call:
```python
import sympy as sp, itertools, math, collections, sys, json, random, math, functools, fractions, decimal, fractions, collections, itertools, math
x=sp.Symbol('x')
def divisors_of_one_plus_xd(d):
    # factor polynomial over Z
    f = sp.expand(1 + x**d)
    fac = sp.factor(f, modulus=None)  # factor over integers
    # sympy factor returns product of irreducible factors
    # need to get list of irreducible factors with multiplicities
    # we can use sp.factor_list
    faclist = sp.factor_list(f)[1]  # returns [(factor,exp), ...]
    # Each factor is primitive with integer coefficients
    factors = [ (sp.expand(fac[0]), fac[1]) for fac in faclist ]
    # Since all exponents are 1 (squarefree), but we handle general.
    # Generate all subsets of factors (including empty -> 1)
    all_divisors=set()
    # We'll produce monic divisors? Not necessarily monic; but each factor might have leading coefficient 1.
    # Multiply chosen factors (with multiplicity 1)
    for r in range(len(factors)+1):
        for comb in itertools.combinations(factors, r):
            poly = sp.Integer(1)
            for fpoly,exp in comb:
                poly = sp.expand(poly * fpoly)
            all_divisors.add(sp.expand(poly))
    return all_divisors

def compute_D(maxd=30):
    D=set()
    for d in range(1, maxd+1):
        for p in divisors_of_one_plus_xd(d):
            deg = sp.degree(p)
            if deg <= 8:
                # primitive: gcd of coefficients =1
                coeffs = sp.Poly(p, x).all_coeffs()
                # compute gcd of ints
                g=0
                for c in coeffs:
                    g=math.gcd(g, int(abs(c)))
                if g==1:  # primitive (including ±1)
                    # store canonical representation: maybe as tuple of coeffs
                    D.add(sp.expand(p))
    return D

D=compute_D(30)
len(D)

```python
def min_d_for_poly(poly):
    # Find smallest d>0 such that (1+x**d) % poly == 0 (as integer polynomial)
    for d in range(1,200):
        if sp.rem(1 + x**d, poly, domain=sp.ZZ) == 0:
            return d
    return None
min_d={}
for p in D2:
    min_d[p]=min_d_for_poly(p)
min_d

Better, we can reason analytically: Condition implies α must be primitive divisor of some 1 + x^d times monomial factor; these are precisely the primitive divisors with constant term ±1 (since α must have gcd 1) and degree ≤8 after shift. So classification is rigorous.

But we should provide a rigorous reasoning: Show that if α is shifty, then there exists d such that its generating polynomial divides 1 + x^d (up to monomial). Conversely, if α's generating polynomial is of that form, we can construct β. So classification.

Let's outline proof.

Let α ∈ ℱ be shifty with existence of β and distinct k,l. Define d = l - k >0. Consider generating functions: A(x) = Σ α(t) x^{t}, B(x) = Σ β(t) x^{t}. Then shift S_n(α) corresponds to multiplication by x^{n} in generating function: S_n(α)(t) = α(t+n); then its inner product with β equals coefficient of x^{?}. Let's compute: S_n(α)⋆β = Σ_t α(t+n) β(t) = coefficient of x^{?}. The convolution A(x) * B(x) = Σ_{n} ( Σ_t α(t) β(n-t)) x^{n}. But Σ_t α(t+n) β(t) = Σ_t α(t) β(t-n) = coefficient of x^{-n} in A(x) * B_rev? Let's be careful.

Define generating functions: A(x) = Σ_{i} α(i) x^{i}. Define B(x) = Σ_{j} β(j) x^{-j}. Then product: A(x) B(x) = Σ_i Σ_j α(i) β(j) x^{i - j}. Coefficient of x^{n} is Σ_i α(i) β(i-n) = Σ_t α(t+n) β(t) (by substituting i = t+n). Thus coefficient of x^{n} equals S_n(α)⋆β. Good. So condition is that A(x) B(x) = x^{k} + x^{l} = x^{k}(1 + x^{d}) where B(x) is a Laurent polynomial with integer coefficients.

Thus A(x) divides x^{k}(1 + x^{d}) in ℤ[x, x^{-1}] with unit ±x^{m}. Since A(x) has support between 0 and 8, it can be written as x^{s}·P(x) where s = minimal exponent (≥0) and P(x) has constant term ≠0. Since A(x) has integer coefficients and finite support, it is a polynomial (not Laurent). Then the factorization implies P(x) divides 1 + x^{d} in ℤ[x].

Thus classification: α is shifty iff there exists s (0 ≤ s ≤ 8) and primitive divisor P of 1 + x^{d} (some d) with degree ≤8 - s, such that α = x^{s}·P (or negative). So number of shifty α equals 2× Σ_{P primitive divisor} (9 - deg(P)), where we count all possible shifts within length 9.

Now compute the set of primitive divisors of 1 + x^{d} with degree ≤8. This set equals the set of all nonconstant divisors of cyclotomic polynomials (or product). Equivalent to all integer primitive polynomials dividing 1 + x^{d} with degree ≤8. We can compute them via factoring of 1 + x^{d} for d up to 30; we found 22 such distinct polynomials (including constant 1). Then total shifty α = 2 * Σ_{P} (9 - deg(P)) = 160.

Now we need to double-check inclusion of constant 1 (degree 0). This yields shifts 0..8 (9 possibilities). That gives 9 α functions: α(t) = ±1 at a single position s (and zeros elsewhere). Indeed those correspond to α = ±x^s (i.e., monomial). Let's verify these are shifty: α is monomial; we need β such that convolution yields two spikes. ### Turn 6 - Model Response:
analysisThe computation timed out maybe due to large enumeration. Let's try to compute incrementally or smarter.

We can compute all possible primitive divisors up to degree 8 of 1 + x^d for d up to, say, 200, but maybe we can do using factorization for each d and collect factors. Let's try optimized: iterate d up to 200, factor 1 + x^d using sympy factor_list which may be okay. Let's attempt with moderate bound 200.assistantanalysis to=python codedef compute_D_upto(maxd):
    D=set()
    for d in range(1, maxd+1):
        # factor using cyclotomic factorization: sympy.factor_list may be heavy; but okay for up to 200.
        poly = 1 + x**d
        factors = sp.factor_list(poly, modulus=None)[1]  # list of (factor,exp)
        # each factor is irreducible cyclotomic polynomial
        # generate all products of subsets:
        # there are at most few factors per d.
        # we can just add each factor and also all their products.
        # We'll use recursion.
        def add_subsets(idx, current):
            if idx==len(factors):
                D.add(sp.expand(current))
                return
            f,_ = factors[idx]
            # not include
            add_subsets(idx+1, current)
            # include
            add_subsets(idx+1, current*f)
        add_subsets(0, sp.Integer(1))
    # Filter degree <=8 and primitive
    D_filtered=set()
    for p in D:
        deg = sp.degree(p)
        if deg<=8:
            coeffs = sp.Poly(p, x).all_coeffs()
            g=0
            for c in coeffs:
                g=math.gcd(g, int(abs(c)))
            if g==1:
                D_filtered.add(sp.expand(p))
    return D_filtered

D200 = compute_D_upto(200)
len(D200)

def compute_D_upto(maxd):
    D=set()
    for d in range(1, maxd+1):
        # factor using cyclotomic factorization: sympy.factor_list may be heavy; but okay for up to 200.
        poly = 1 + x**d
        factors = sp.factor_list(poly, modulus=None)[1]  # list of (factor,exp)
        # each factor is irreducible cyclotomic polynomial
        # generate all products of subsets:
        # there are at most few factors per d.
        # we can just add each factor and also all their products.
        # We'll use recursion.
        def add_subsets(idx, current):
            if idx==len(factors):
                D.add(sp.expand(current))
                return
            f,_ = factors[idx]
            # not include
            add_subsets(idx+1, current)
            # include
            add_subsets(idx+1, current*f)
        add_subsets(0, sp.Integer(1))
    # Filter degree <=8 and primitive
    D_filtered=set()
    for p in D:
        deg = sp.degree(p)
        if deg<=8:
            coeffs = sp.Poly(p, x).all_coeffs()
            g=0
            for c in coeffs:
                g=math.gcd(g, int(abs(c)))
            if g==1:
                D_filtered.add(sp.expand(p))
    return D_filtered

This essentially requires all n_i have the same exponent of 2 (i.e., v2(n_i) is equal for all i) because they must be of the form 2^{a+1} * s_i with same a for all i. If n_i have different v2 values, you cannot find a single d.

### Turn 8 - Model Response:
analysisThus answer 160.

Let's also verify via constructing all α functions via classification and testing with algorithm to compute β (by solving linear equations) and confirm they work.

We'll generate all α's from D list with shifts and signs, and for each compute d = minimal d for polynomial divisor (the minimal d we earlier found) and construct β as B(x) = x^{k - s} * (1 + x^d)/P(x). Choose k = s (so k-s=0) for simplicity; then B(x) = (1 + x^d)/P(x). However if we choose k = s, product becomes x^s * (1 + x^d) (since A = x^s * P, then A*B = x^s * P * ((1 + x^d)/P) = x^s (1 + x^d) = x^{k} (1 + x^d) with k = s. Good. So set k=s. Then l = s + d. That yields distinct k,l.

Thus we can compute β(t) coefficients and verify that convolution matches condition.

We'll implement for each α and verify.assistantanalysis to=python codedef poly_to_coeffs(poly):
    # returns dict exponent->coeff
    p = sp.Poly(poly, x)
    coeffs = p.all_coeffs()
    deg = p.degree()
    # poly is sum_{i=0}^{deg} coeffs[i] x^{deg - i}
    dct = {}
    for i, c in enumerate(coeffs):
        exp = deg - i
        if c != 0:
            dct[exp] = int(c)
    return dct

def convolution(alpha, beta):
    # compute product of generating functions as described
    # both dict exponent->coeff (could have negative exponents)
    res={}
    for i,ai in alpha.items():
        for j,bj in beta.items():
            exp=i+j
            res[exp]=res.get(exp,0)+ai*bj
    return res

def test_alpha(alpha_dict):
    # Find minimal s (least exponent)
    exps=[e for e in alpha_dict if alpha_dict[e]!=0]
    s=min(exps)
    # extract polynomial P = alpha/x^s
    P = {e-s:coeff for e,coeff in alpha_dict.items() if coeff!=0}
    # Convert to sympy poly to find d such that P divides 1+x^d.
    # We'll compute minimal d by scanning.
    # We'll convert P to sympy polynomial.
    Ppoly = sum(coeff*x**exp for exp,coeff in P.items())
    # search for d up to maybe 30
    for d in range(1,101):
        if sp.rem(1 + x**d, Ppoly, domain=sp.ZZ) == 0:
            # compute B = (1 + x^d)/Ppoly
            Bpoly = sp.expand((1 + x**d) // Ppoly)
            # Build dict for B (exponents)
            Bdict = poly_to_coeffs(Bpoly)
            # compute product alpha*B
            prod=convolution(alpha_dict, Bdict)
            # Check if product is x^s + x^{s+d}
            expected = {s:1, s+d:1}
            # All other coefficients zero
            if all(prod.get(e,0)==expected.get(e,0) for e in set(prod.keys()).union(expected.keys())):
                return True
    return False

def generate_all_alpha():
    alphas=[]
    for p in D2:
        deg=sp.degree(p)
        coeffs = poly_to_coeffs(p)
        for s in range(0,9-deg):
            # shift
            for sign in [1,-1]:
                a={exp+s: sign*coeff for exp,coeff in coeffs.items()}
                alphas.append(a)
    return alphas

alphas=generate_all_alpha()
len(alphas)

def poly_to_coeffs(poly):
    # returns dict exponent->coeff
    p = sp.Poly(poly, x)
    coeffs = p.all_coeffs()
    deg = p.degree()
    # poly is sum_{i=0}^{deg} coeffs[i] x^{deg - i}
    dct = {}
    for i, c in enumerate(coeffs):
        exp = deg - i
        if c != 0:
            dct[exp] = int(c)
    return dct

def convolution(alpha, beta):
    # compute product of generating functions as described
    # both dict exponent->coeff (could have negative exponents)
    res={}
    for i,ai in alpha.items():
        for j,bj in beta.items():
            exp=i+j
            res[exp]=res.get(exp,0)+ai*bj
    return res

def test_alpha(alpha_dict):
    # Find minimal s (least exponent)
    exps=[e for e in alpha_dict if alpha_dict[e]!=0]
    s=min(exps)
    # extract polynomial P = alpha/x^s
    P = {e-s:coeff for e,coeff in alpha_dict.items() if coeff!=0}
    # Convert to sympy poly to find d such that P divides 1+x^d.
    # We'll compute minimal d by scanning.
    # We'll convert P to sympy polynomial.
    Ppoly = sum(coeff*x**exp for exp,coeff in P.items())
    # search for d up to maybe 30
    for d in range(1,101):
        if sp.rem(1 + x**d, Ppoly, domain=sp.ZZ) == 0:
            # compute B = (1 + x^d)/Ppoly
            Bpoly = sp.expand((1 + x**d) // Ppoly)
            # Build dict for B (exponents)
            Bdict = poly_to_coeffs(Bpoly)
            # compute product alpha*B
            prod=convolution(alpha_dict, Bdict)
            # Check if product is x^s + x^{s+d}
            expected = {s:1, s+d:1}
            # All other coefficients zero
            if all(prod.get(e,0)==expected.get(e,0) for e in set(prod.keys()).union(expected.keys())):
                return True
    return False

def generate_all_alpha():
    alphas=[]
    for p in D2:
        deg=sp.degree(p)
        coeffs = poly_to_coeffs(p)
        for s in range(0,9-deg):
            # shift
            for sign in [1,-1]:
                a={exp+s: sign*coeff for exp,coeff in coeffs.items()}
                alphas.append(a)
    return alphas

alphas=generate_all_alpha()
len(alphas)

assistantanalysis to=python codedef test_alpha(alpha_dict):
    exps=[e for e in alpha_dict if alpha_dict[e]!=0]
    s=min(exps)
    # Build P poly
    P = {e-s:coeff for e,coeff in alpha_dict.items() if coeff!=0}
    Ppoly = sum(coeff*x**exp for exp,coeff in P.items())
    for d in range(1,200):
        # check divisibility
        if sp.rem(1 + x**d, Ppoly, domain=sp.ZZ) == 0:
            # quotient:
            Qpoly = sp.expand(sp.quo(1 + x**d, Ppoly))
            # Build B dict (beta) as Qpoly (no shift)
            Bdict = poly_to_coeffs(Qpoly)
            # compute product
            prod=convolution(alpha_dict, Bdict)
            # expected two spikes at s and s+d

            # check all entries

                if prod.get(exp,0)!=expected.get(exp,0):
                    ok=False
                    break

    return False

### Turn 11 - Model Response:
analysisAll generated α work.

Now check that there are no other α within coefficient bounds [-3,3] not captured but also shifty.

We'll brute-force enumerate all α with coefficients in range -3..3 and test for existence of β (by scanning d up to maybe 30). That's many combos 7^9 ~ 40 million which is large. But we can prune using necessary condition constant term after factoring monomial must be ±1 and primitive. So we could enumerate using condition. But we have already enumerated all α that satisfy classification; but to cross-check, we can search over small coefficient magnitude but random sampling maybe.

But we are confident.

Thus final answer: 160.

We should produce explanation.

Let's craft solution:

- Represent α, β as generating functions A(x), B(x) = Σ α(t) x^{t}, Σ β(t) x^{-t}, product equals Σ_n (S_n(α)⋆β) x^{n}.
- Shifty condition translates to A(x) B(x) = x^{k} + x^{l} = x^{k} (1 + x^{d}) where d = l - k >0.
- Therefore A(x) divides x^{k} (1 + x^{d}) in ℤ[x, x^{-1}]. Since A has support inside [0,8], we write A(x) = x^{s} P(x) where s = min exponent; then P(0) ≠ 0. Hence P(x) divides 1 + x^{d}. Also P must be primitive (coeffs gcd 1) because constant term of 1 + x^{d} is 1.
- Conversely, any such factor yields a shifty α (choose β from the quotient).
- So classification reduces to primitive divisors of polynomials 1 + x^{d} of degree ≤8 (because deg P = deg A - s ≤8) .
- Determine all such divisors: they are exactly the 22 polynomials listed (including 1). (We can argue using cyclotomic factorization: 1 + x^{d} = ∏_{n | 2d, n ∤ d} Φ_n(x) where Φ_n are cyclotomic polynomials. Each divisor corresponds to a product of distinct Φ_n with the property that all n have the same exact 2‑adic valuation; because they must all divide 2d but not d.) Checking all n with φ(n) ≤ 8 gives a finite list; the resulting products yield the 22 polynomials.

- For each divisor P of degree r, we can shift it by any s = 0,…, 8−r, obtaining α(x) = ± x^{s} P(x). Different shifts give different functions; the sign gives another. Hence for each divisor of degree r we obtain 2·(9−r) shifty functions.

- Summing over all divisors we obtain total number = 2·[ (9−0)·1 + (9−1)·1 + (9−2)·2 + (9−3)·1 + (9−4)·3 + (9−5)·1 + (9−6)·4 + (9−7)·3 + (9−8)·6 ] = 2·80 = 160.

Thus there are $\boxed{160}$ shifty functions.

We might also optionally list the 22 base polynomials.

Now produce final answer accordingly.assistantfinalLet  

\[
A(x)=\sum_{t\in\mathbb Z}\alpha(t)x^{t},\qquad 
B(x)=\sum_{t\in\mathbb Z}\beta(t)x^{-t}\qquad(\alpha ,\beta\in\mathcal F).
\

---
